  //<![CDATA[
    function loadScript(url, callback) {
      var script = document.createElement("script");
      script.type = "text/javascript";
    
      if (script.readyState) {  //Internet Explorer
          script.onreadystatechange = function() {
            if (script.readyState == "loaded" ||
                    script.readyState == "complete") {
              script.onreadystatechange = null;
              callback();
            }
          };
      } else {  //Other browsers
        script.onload = function() {
          callback();
        };
      }
    
      script.src = url;
      document.getElementsByTagName("head")[0].appendChild(script);
    }
    
    function initAppleId() {
      AppleID.auth.init({
        clientId : 'com.goodreads.app', 
        scope : 'name email',
        redirectURI: 'https://www.goodreads.com/apple_users/sign_in_with_apple_web',
        state: 'apple_oauth_state_fd9f107a-4fd3-4354-932e-b2545db915e0'
      });

      var APPLE_SIGN_IN_JS_URL =  "https://appleid.cdn-apple.com/appleauth/static/jsapi/appleid/1/en_US/appleid.auth.js"

<div id="review_1831067568" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1831067568" />
    <a title="Manny" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1713956-manny"><img alt="Manny" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1442491692p2/1713956.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1831067568?book_show_action=true">Dec 08, 2016</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Manny" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Manny" href="/user/show/1713956-manny">Manny</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Hardcover 9788252525649" href="/book/show/31819767-pan">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1713956-manny?shelf=too-sexy-for-maiden-aunts">too-sexy-for-maiden-aunts</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1713956-manny?shelf=why-not-call-it-poetry">why-not-call-it-poetry</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1713956-manny?shelf=swedish-norwegian-and-danish">swedish-norwegian-and-danish</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1713956-manny?shelf=parody-homage">parody-homage</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1713956-manny?shelf=older-men-younger-women">older-men-younger-women</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1713956-manny?shelf=translation-is-impossible">translation-is-impossible</a>
          </div>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1831067568" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer16354252712183626287">I went into the café and sat down in the corner. I prefer to sit there. I took out my book and opened it. After a while, I looked up and saw that one of the waitresses was standing behind me. I realised she had been there for some time. She was a pretty brunette who looked about twenty.<br/><br/>- Excuse me, I said. I didn't notice you.<br/><br/>- That's okay, she replied and laughed. I didn't want to disturb you. What's that you've got there?<br/><br/>- <i>Pan</i>, I said. By Knut Hamsun.<br/><br/>- And you can read it? she said. What langu</span>
  <span id="freeText16354252712183626287" style="display:none">I went into the café and sat down in the corner. I prefer to sit there. I took out my book and opened it. After a while, I looked up and saw that one of the waitresses was standing behind me. I realised she had been there for some time. She was a pretty brunette who looked about twenty.<br /><br />- Excuse me, I said. I didn't notice you.<br /><br />- That's okay, she replied and laughed. I didn't want to disturb you. What's that you've got there?<br /><br />- <i>Pan</i>, I said. By Knut Hamsun.<br /><br />- And you can read it? she said. What language is it in?<br /><br />- I suppose Norwegian, I said. Or maybe Danish. <br /><br />- You can't tell the difference? she said and raised her eyebrows. <br /><br />- I expressed myself badly, I said. They were almost the same language in 1894. At least in novels.<br /><br />- But you can read it? she asked again.<br /><br />- Quite easily, I said. If I guess a few words. It's very beautiful. He has a unique way of writing. I wanted to tell her more about Hamsun's style, but she interrupted me.<br /><br />- So what are you doing later today? she asked.<br /><br />- Finishing this book, I said. I like to read a lot. She was standing too close to me. I shifted my position and stretched out my hand, knocking over a glass of water.<br /><br />- I'm terribly sorry, I said. I was so embarrassed by my clumsiness that tears almost came into my eyes.<br /><br />- It doesn't matter, she told me. She fetched a rag and mopped up the mess I'd made. <br /><br />- Now what can I get you, she asked. A coffee? And I'll leave you alone with your book.<br /><br />- A latte, I said. A few minutes later, she came back. She put down the coffee on the table with exaggerated care.<br /><br />- You see, I'm not disturbing you, she said, and she patted my hand.<br /><br />I sat and tried to read <i>Pan</i>, but I was unable to concentrate. I drank my coffee slowly and tried to think how to explain the charm of the writing. I realised that I didn't even know the waitress's name. After a while, I walked over to the counter. She was talking with an older man who looked like her boss.<br /><br />- Coffee okay? she said. That's $4.10. I handed her a five. She gave back me the change. There was a little jar of coins in front of me with a sign saying BEER MONEY. I put my ninety cents in it. <br /><br />- Thanks, she smiled.<br /><br />- You know, I said, speaking a bit too quickly, I believe I can tell you what's special about Hamsun. <br /><br />- Who? she said.<br /><br />- The Norwegian who wrote the book I'm reading, I said.<br /><br />- Oh, right, she said. <br /><br />- It's the way he has of describing people, I explained. What they feel and what they see. You see, he can kind of--<br /><br />- Sorry, she said. I'm in the middle of doing something with Mac here. Why don't you come back and tell me tomorrow?<br /><br />I suddenly hated her. She had only pretended to be interested. I reached into the jar and took back my ninety cents. Her face crumpled as though I'd just slapped her. I immediately regretted what I had done.<br /><br />- Oh, I'm sorry, I'm sorry, I'm so sorry, I said. I'm sorry. Please forgive me. Can you forgive me?<br /><br />- It's okay, she said after a few seconds. She tried to laugh, but I could see her boss staring at me. <br /><br />I left without saying goodbye.<br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="16354252712183626287" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_2689325410" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2689325410" />
    <a title="Jim Fonseca" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/6743601-jim-fonseca"><img alt="Jim Fonseca" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1397669173p2/6743601.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2689325410?book_show_action=true">Jan 25, 2019</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Jim Fonseca" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Jim Fonseca" href="/user/show/6743601-jim-fonseca">Jim Fonseca</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/6743601-jim-fonseca?shelf=psychological-novel">psychological-novel</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/6743601-jim-fonseca?shelf=norwegian-authors">norwegian-authors</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2689325410" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer7178545150263852691">First published in 1894, Pan is an early example of the influence of psychology upon literature – certainly early for Scandinavian writers. The book is translated from the Norwegian. For the author this emphasis on psychology is very deliberate. Hamsun wrote to a friend and critic about his best-known book, Hunger (1890), “If we add it up, I don’t think you’d find more psychical emotions in Raskolnikov than in my book.” <br/><br/><img src='https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1548469655i/26971595._SX540_.jpg' width='363' height='242' alt='description' class='gr-hostedUserImg'/><br/><br/>In Hamsun’s case psychology shows up primarily as impulsiveness for reasons </span>
  <span id="freeText7178545150263852691" style="display:none">First published in 1894, Pan is an early example of the influence of psychology upon literature – certainly early for Scandinavian writers. The book is translated from the Norwegian. For the author this emphasis on psychology is very deliberate. Hamsun wrote to a friend and critic about his best-known book, Hunger (1890), “If we add it up, I don’t think you’d find more psychical emotions in Raskolnikov than in my book.” <br><br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1548469655i/26971595._SX540_.jpg" width="363" height="242" alt="description" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><br>In Hamsun’s case psychology shows up primarily as impulsiveness for reasons unknown to the actor. First, shortly after she has met the main character, the girl kisses him full on the lips in front of all the church ladies at a picnic – that raises eyebrows in a small village in mid-1800’s Norway. (Although a 28-year old man having an interest in a 15-year-old girl was apparently ok back then, even to her father, who encouraged the relationship. Later in the book we learn she lies about her age and may be older.) At another community event, two boats full of people are rowing back from another picnic on an island and he impulsively grabs her shoe and throws it into the water. He has no idea why he did this. Later, alone in his cabin, he impulsively shoots himself in the foot. She, in a fury for no obvious reason, tramples on a brooch another suitor had given her. Later he detonates an explosion that dislodges a huge rock, overturning a boat that results in someone’s death. (There are two murders in the book disguised as accidents.) <br><br>The other way psychology shows up is in the mutual stand-off-ishness of the young woman and the man who obviously love each other but seem to think that showing disinterest and even distain for the other will somehow ‘bring them around.’ So they avoid each other, insult each other, flirt with other people, and never get their act together. At one point he thinks “It occurred to me, moreover, that I had perhaps got closer to her precisely by treating her with scorn and indifference.” <br><br>The main character is a military officer living for a year in a rural hut in far northern Norway near the Arctic Circle. He must be on leave since he has no duties. He leads a primitive life, hunts with his dog every day, and communes with nature – a very Waldenesque existence: “The monotonous sighing of the wind and the familiar trees and stones mean much to me; I feel a strange sense of gratitude, everything reached out toward me, blends with me, I love all things.” Pan of the title is the half-goat Greek god of the wild, of nature, shepherds, flocks, mountains and the companion of nymphs. <br><br>The story is structured a bit like Russian dolls. It starts out as a memoir written by the main character “for his own amusement” a couple of years after the events. Why you would write about killing someone with an explosion is a psychological study in itself. And there is an epilogue, telling of the main character’s tragic death, written by a ‘friend’ who found the manuscript, and who says of the now-deceased main character: “[He] was in many ways an unusual and likeable man. In justice to him I must admit this, in spite of the fact that in my soul I still feel hostility toward [him] and the memory of him rouses my hatred.”<br><br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1548469466i/26971585._SY540_.jpg" width="242" height="363" alt="description" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><br>Hamsun won the Noble Prize in 1920. He’s best known for his work Hunger, about a poverty-stricken writer wandering through Oslo looking for his next meal. But Hamsun is not honored very much in Norway due to his collaboration with the Nazis during their occupation of the country in WW II. He actually met with Hitler, who thought of Hamsun as a ‘genius.’ Although the meeting turned into a shouting match, the author’s reputation never recovered in his home country and some Norwegians still consider him a traitor. (There’s a good article about Hamsun in the Dec. 26, 2005 Atlantic: “In From the Cold” by Jeffrey Frank from which I have taken some of the thoughts in this review.) <br><br>Actually a pretty good read for something written in 1894. Perhaps it’s the translation but it didn’t seem stilted or old-fashioned in writing style. A good but strange story. <br><br>Photo of Skudeneshavn, Norway from blog.routeperfect.com<br>Painting of the author by Alfredo Andersen from Wikipedia <br></span>
  <a data-text-id="7178545150263852691" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <div class="friendReviews elementListBrown" >
    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_253995523" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/253995523" />
    <a title="s.penkevich" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/6431467-s-penkevich"><img alt="s.penkevich" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1587089836p2/6431467.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/253995523?book_show_action=true">Dec 31, 2011</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="s.penkevich" class="user" itemprop="url" name="s.penkevich" href="/user/show/6431467-s-penkevich">s.penkevich</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>


          <div><span class="uitext greyText">Recommends it for: </span>
           <span class="uitext reviewText">The lovers, the dreamers, and me</span></div>

          <div>
    <span class="greyText">Recommended to s.penkevich by: </span>
      Bukowski
  </div>


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/6431467-s-penkevich?shelf=nobel-prize-winners">nobel-prize-winners</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/6431467-s-penkevich?shelf=europe">europe</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/6431467-s-penkevich?shelf=nature">nature</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/6431467-s-penkevich?shelf=hamsun">hamsun</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer253995523" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer2227190375135727648"><i>-I love a dream of love I once had, I love you, and I love this patch of earth.<br/>-And which do you love best?<br/>-The dream.</i><br/><br/>With his succinct 1894 novel, <u>Pan</u>, Knut Hamsun once again displays his prowess of capturing the human psychology and detailing the internal conflicts that arise through the sudden rise and fall of moods. Through Glahn, the capricious man who has taken up residence within the northern wilderness and the socialite Edvarda, Hamsun demonstrates how even the slightest romantic collisi</span>
  <span id="freeText2227190375135727648" style="display:none"><i>-I love a dream of love I once had, I love you, and I love this patch of earth.<br>-And which do you love best?<br>-The dream.</i><br><br>With his succinct 1894 novel, <u>Pan</u>, Knut Hamsun once again displays his prowess of capturing the human psychology and detailing the internal conflicts that arise through the sudden rise and fall of moods. Through Glahn, the capricious man who has taken up residence within the northern wilderness and the socialite Edvarda, Hamsun demonstrates how even the slightest romantic collision of two souls can create a spark whose burn is felt long after the romance has begun to smolder. The irrationalities that exist in such a torrid affair between man and woman come alive in his prose and fiery dialogue as the reader experiences the pains and paranoia of chasing after an elusive lover.<br><br>Appearing in print only two years after <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/32586.Mysteries" title="Mysteries by Knut Hamsun" rel="nofollow noopener">Mysteries</a>, an incredible account of an irrational stranger much like Glahn (and my favorite of his books), Hamsun appears much more matured, confidant and focused in his writing. While <u>Mysteries</u> was slightly bloated, <u>Pan</u> is trimmed down to a potent simplicity and directness. Returning to the first person narration similar to <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/32585.Hunger" title="Hunger by Knut Hamsun" rel="nofollow noopener">Hunger</a>, the reader is treated to a troubled and unreliable mind to lead them through this tragic love story. The first section of the novel is Glahn’s journal, which he insists was purely ‘<i> to while away the time</i>’ and amuse himself, shows him reflecting on the events of a previous summer. While he repeatedly mentions that Edvarda no longer crosses his mind, he continuously winds his way back to her in his accounts. The second section, written from an acquaintance of Glahn’s a year a short time after the journal, offers a slightly different image of the man. While Glahn portrays himself as socially inept, unattractive and clumsy, the second narrator displays him as a powerful foreign force in the world with a charm and chiseled looks that make him irresistible to women.<br><br>As the title would imply, this is a story of the forest with Glahn as a ‘Pan’ of sorts. He is a perfect blend of man and nature, coexisting with the wild in perfect harmony. While he is a hunter, he only kills what he must for bare survival, never taking more than what he needs to eat in the immediate future. He is also seemingly superhumanly in-tuned with nature, being able to tell the exact time through the flowers and trees and seemingly able to commune with the land on which he dwells. While his descriptions of society and the people he interacts with are exceedingly simplistic, the true prose of the novel blossoms in his depictions of nature and the reader feels the glowing sun, hears the rushing waters and smells the trees and grass through his words. In a novel of love and lust, the intimate scenes between him and his various lovers occur primarily offstage while the sexual imagery is reserved for nature:<br><blockquote>’<i>In the night hours of the forest, great white flowers have suddenly opened out, their chalices spread wide, and they breathe. And furry hawk-moths bury themselves in their petals and set the whole plant quivering. I go from flower to flower; they are in ecstasy, and I see their intoxication.</i>’</blockquote><br>He is Hamsun’s image of pure masculinity and a ideal blend of both creative and destructive powers, a mythical being both beast and man. Hamsun decorates the novel with allusions to fables, the most obvious the name of Glahn’s dog, <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/12452.Aesop" title="Aesop" rel="nofollow noopener">Aesop</a>, to help bring this idea of Pan alive in a realistic setting.<br><br>If Glahn is a symbol of the wild, then Edvarda is a symbol of society. She is well of high standings both financially and socially, playing hostess to many soirees and moving between multiple suitors. The pair are doomed from the start as Hamsun illustrates the infinite divide between well groomed society and the primal realm of the wild. Glahn is at ease in the forest, yet feels completely awkward and ill at ease in social settings and displays bizarre behavior and strange outbursts. Edvarda is a creature of irrationality as well, playing men off of one another and escaping into the wilderness in secret to satisfy more basic instincts of passions under the cover of night. <br><br>The frustration felt by Glahn as he grapples with his passion practically chokes the reader. He is just as in-tune with the body language of others as he is with nature, and is constantly analyzing Edvarda in social settings. In the forest, she goes wild with emotion, yet in society she is cold, calculating and inaccessible. She dismisses his advances yet keeps him on the line with one sweet smile or insistence that he be the last to leave, yet refuses to let on to any romantic entanglement in the company of others. It is a blockage of passion that crushes souls and forces one to act out if only to be acknowledged. Always being one with nature, the romance is played out with the seasons with fall darkening the tone of the novel and ushering us towards certain chilly doom and destruction. <br><br>Never has there been an author whose words have sung better harmony to the melody of my soul as Hamsun. His characters come alive wonderfully through his careful prose and marvelously plotted out shifts and moods. This is a novel for anyone who has loved, anyone who has lost, and anyone who has squirmed in frustration over a ill-fated tryst. Despite the meager size of the novel, <u>Pan</u> is another knock out and delivers just as much emotional impact and literary brilliance as Hamsun’s other novels while being much warmer and heartfelt than his previous works while setting the grounds for a further investigation of the rift between nature and society that he explores in <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/342049.Growth_of_the_Soil" title="Growth of the Soil by Knut Hamsun" rel="nofollow noopener">Growth of the Soil</a>. Satisfy your primal instincts and enter the dark forest of Hamsun’s mind. The dream of love always burns brighter than the loves we use to bandage our wounds.<br><br><b>4.5/5</b><br><br>A quick note on the translation<br>I read the James W. McFarlane translation, an older translation published by Noonday (of which Goodreads seems not to have in their various editions). While it still delivers, I would direct anyone towards a different translator. The newer Penguin edition would probably be a better choice. While Hamsun’s poetic might is strong enough to punch through, I felt I was missing some of the flow that I glimpsed in his other books. It was as if McFarlane was hitting all the right notes, but not letting them truly flow.<br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1380366049i/718254.jpg" class="gr-hostedUserImg"></span>
  <a data-text-id="2227190375135727648" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <div class="friendReviews elementListBrown" >
    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_2427965472" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2427965472" />
    <a title="Steven Godin" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/26706841-steven-godin"><img alt="Steven Godin" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1605181037p2/26706841.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2427965472?book_show_action=true">Jun 23, 2018</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Steven Godin" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Steven Godin" href="/user/show/26706841-steven-godin">Steven Godin</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/26706841-steven-godin?shelf=scandinavia-iceland">scandinavia-iceland</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/26706841-steven-godin?shelf=classic-fiction">classic-fiction</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2427965472" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer15257363047991767525">Writing of his new novel in a letter from Paris, in 1893, Knut Hamsun noted, "My new book will be beautiful; it takes place in Nordland, a quiet and red love story. There will be no polemics in it, just people under different skies." He did indeed live up to his letter, it's precisely that.<br/>The Scandinavian wilderness comes alive so vividly, and there is something in the narrative that almost makes it read like a mystical fable, or even a dream within a dream. <br/><br/>Set deep in the forests of Norway, i</span>
  <span id="freeText15257363047991767525" style="display:none">Writing of his new novel in a letter from Paris, in 1893, Knut Hamsun noted, "My new book will be beautiful; it takes place in Nordland, a quiet and red love story. There will be no polemics in it, just people under different skies." He did indeed live up to his letter, it's precisely that.<br />The Scandinavian wilderness comes alive so vividly, and there is something in the narrative that almost makes it read like a mystical fable, or even a dream within a dream. <br /><br />Set deep in the forests of Norway, it follows Lt. Glahn in a deeply psychological form as he struggles to juggle his unity with nature and the feelings and experiences that defines humanity. Told in the first person he comes across as a figure of immense complexity. Enigmatic, intriguing, and carrying within him a deep melancholy. First off, I loved the straightforward narration, it's lyrical and beautifully told, sometimes in the present tense, sometimes contemplative and poetic. The story is breathtaking in its simplicity , but also strangely compelling, and so effective. Like Hamsun's words are growing from tiny seeds sewn into the earth, leaving a trail on the forest floor for the reader to follow.<br /><br />Glahn is a hunter who with his dog, lives in a small hut buried in the Norwegian woods. He's closer to nature than he is to culture, and his descriptions of the natural world turns the forest into a character itself. "....I'm filled with a mysterious gratitude; everything befriends me, intermingles with me, I love all things. I pick up a dry twig, hold it in my hand and look at it as I sit there having my own thoughts. The twig is nearly rotten, its poor bark affects me, pity stirring my heart. And when I get up to go, I do not throw the twig away but lay it down and stand there feeling sorry for it." There are some achingly beautiful passages of writing, especially when Glahn is in solitude, and he is a bundle of changing emotions, with his soul rising before sinking again into a dark mood. He may have compassion for the surrounding landscapes, but when it comes to people, it's a different story. He would end up in an affair with a young woman, Eva, but shows little genuine affection for her. Like he would rather be off in the woods, making love to a log.<br /><br />There is also the character of Edvarda, who is somewhat shadowy, almost like a figure seen through a slightly opaque veil. She seems unreal, and the motives behind most of her actions are inexplicable. Between the lines, there seems to be a battle between the sexes. Glahn being romantically awkward, expresses his passions for Edvarda in a way that is both fervent and hateful. You kind of want him to disappear off into the wilderness and be happy on his own. There is something in the way Hamsun writes that compels his reader onward, a spellbinding effect that not many writers can reach. Pan could be read many times, and probably should, each time retaining all its seductive charm from that first encounter. <br /><br />By its finale, things are complicated somewhat, casting more light on Glahn's apparent self-destructiveness. You kind of start to realise that he is a rather unsavory character, the sorrow and likable tendencies from earlier on starts to suffer from fatigue. Throughout, the actions of Glahn are sometimes hard to fathom, and his relations with women were frustrating to me as a reader. This clearly isn't meant to read like a conventional story, and even though on the outside it's easy to read, Hamsun does require the reader to think long and hard on what is happening.<br /><br />When in poetic flow with his stunning descriptions of nature, he really does hit the heights, I just would have liked there to be more of it. And at times the other characters mystified me. Having said that, this was my favourite of the four Hamsun books read so far. Just wish I could have had a gathering of trees for company.</span>
  <a data-text-id="15257363047991767525" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_399469692" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/399469692" />
    <a title="WILLIAM2" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/4684009-william2"><img alt="WILLIAM2" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1530030828p2/4684009.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/399469692?book_show_action=true">Aug 24, 2012</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="WILLIAM2" class="user" itemprop="url" name="WILLIAM2" href="/user/show/4684009-william2">WILLIAM2</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4684009-william2?shelf=fiction">fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4684009-william2?shelf=19-ce">19-ce</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4684009-william2?shelf=translation">translation</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer399469692" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer10472986158095231681">As if you needed to revisit it, friends, yet here it is: Hamsun's excruciatingly true-to-life depiction of the exaltation and despair of young love. In his later years, the novelist Anthony Burgess had a pat blurb for certain novels he liked. Of them he would say: "Almost unbearably moving!" That blurb applies perfectly to <i>Pan</i>. This novella is so emotionally affecting! It is so on the money! The reader goes through the entire exhausting emotional cycle here. From initial lusting, to growing inte</span>
  <span id="freeText10472986158095231681" style="display:none">As if you needed to revisit it, friends, yet here it is: Hamsun's excruciatingly true-to-life depiction of the exaltation and despair of young love. In his later years, the novelist Anthony Burgess had a pat blurb for certain novels he liked. Of them he would say: "Almost unbearably moving!" That blurb applies perfectly to <i>Pan</i>. This novella is so emotionally affecting! It is so on the money! The reader goes through the entire exhausting emotional cycle here. From initial lusting, to growing interest, to first titallations of physical contact, to record-breaking Olympic coitus, to a sense of routine and boredom, to the first bickerings of leave taking, to heartbreak, heartbreak, heartbreak and yearning that only makes one's suffering worse. The novella is mercifully short--120 pages. I simply can't imagine 300 pages of this. It's brilliance lies in how it neatly crystallizes the entire range of emotions experienced in erotic love affairs. The magnificent heights of lovemaking, the impossible megalomania of it all, to the lowest lows. That it's set in northern Norway and narrated by a man who lives in a bucolic setting with his hunting dog, all that's interesting too. The man, Lt. Glahn, records his trips into the woods to hunt. There's beautiful description of the Norwegian countryside that reminded me of Per Petterson and Hallidór Laxness, though the latter was an Icelander. Glahn's love object is a silly fickle girl-child called Edvarda. My God, the hatred! The vindictiveness they mete out to each other! Finally, the book is about how such "love" changes us forever. It's a life event for which there is no closure. We become, all of us, the walking wounded. Quite a story. Highly recommended.</span>
  <a data-text-id="10472986158095231681" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_392633145" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/392633145" />
    <a title="Brian" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1938805-brian"><img alt="Brian" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1442608867p2/1938805.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/392633145?book_show_action=true">Aug 15, 2012</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Brian" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Brian" href="/user/show/1938805-brian">Brian</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer392633145" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer11406910458751483055">A beautifully written novel detailing the collision of the natural world and the civilized world over a period of a few seasons in the forests of Norway. Hamsun's prose is at times poetic and magical; several dream-like chapters read like a lyrical mystery. The story keeps you on your toes: a couple of unexpected turns in the narrative had me flipping pages to discover the "what next?". Also: the epilogue (I don't want to call it by its true title in order to not inadvertently divulge a spoiler)</span>
  <span id="freeText11406910458751483055" style="display:none">A beautifully written novel detailing the collision of the natural world and the civilized world over a period of a few seasons in the forests of Norway. Hamsun's prose is at times poetic and magical; several dream-like chapters read like a lyrical mystery. The story keeps you on your toes: a couple of unexpected turns in the narrative had me flipping pages to discover the "what next?". Also: the epilogue (I don't want to call it by its true title in order to not inadvertently divulge a spoiler) is a brilliantly employed device to not only end the novel, but to cast a different light on the protagonist. <br /><br />Bruce Chatwin, in <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/79909.In_Patagonia__Penguin_Classics_" title="In Patagonia (Penguin Classics) by Bruce Chatwin" rel="nofollow noopener">In Patagonia</a>, describes in haunting detail the effect that the barren loneliness of nature can have on some people - so potent that it causes some men to take their own life. Several of Hamsun's descriptions made me feel this desolation: "This silence murmuring in my ears is the blood of all Nature seething"; "Summer nights and still water, and the woods endlessly still. No cry, no footsteps from the road. My heart seemed full as with dark wine"; and "For my place is in the woods, in solitude..."<br /><br />Highly recommended. I will definitely be reading more of Hamsun's work.<br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="11406910458751483055" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<div id="review_997975613" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/997975613" />
    <a title="Whitaker" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1415047-whitaker"><img alt="Whitaker" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1410926609p2/1415047.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/997975613?book_show_action=true">Jul 16, 2014</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Whitaker" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Whitaker" href="/user/show/1415047-whitaker">Whitaker</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was ok"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was ok</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Hardcover " href="/book/show/22027626-pan">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1415047-whitaker?shelf=modern-canon">modern-canon</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1415047-whitaker?shelf=z_2015-read">z_2015-read</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1415047-whitaker?shelf=norway">norway</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1415047-whitaker?shelf=zz-box-09">zz-box-09</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer997975613" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer12263332278755261695"><i>Pan</i> certainly gives me a sense why Knut Hamsun might have been attracted to the Nazi creed. I’m not referring to the anti-Semitism or the crude belief in eugenics, but the belief in the superman and in the purity of nature. Both are deeply apparent in this work, or at least, what I think of this work. <br/><br/>On one level, <i>Pan</i> tells the story—rather trite and clichéd—of a wilful, destructive woman whose self-worth turns on whether she is able to attract a man. Having succeeded, she loses interest and tr</span>
  <span id="freeText12263332278755261695" style="display:none"><i>Pan</i> certainly gives me a sense why Knut Hamsun might have been attracted to the Nazi creed. I’m not referring to the anti-Semitism or the crude belief in eugenics, but the belief in the superman and in the purity of nature. Both are deeply apparent in this work, or at least, what I think of this work. <br /><br />On one level, <i>Pan</i> tells the story—rather trite and clichéd—of a wilful, destructive woman whose self-worth turns on whether she is able to attract a man. Having succeeded, she loses interest and treats him with contempt. He, on the other hand, finds himself utterly helpless before her. <br /><br />However, <i>Pan</i> is clearly intended to be read as more than simply a love story. The protagonist, Thomas Glahn, is rather blatantly a stand-in for the eponymous Pan. He is described as having an animal look; he lives in the woods and is more comfortable being alone in the wilds than being sociable with other people; he is a skilful hunter and lives by shooting game; he wears a leather suit; women find his animal magnetism irresistible. To make sure that you get the symbolism, Hamsun hammers it in by telling us that Glahn’s gunpowder horn has a carving of Pan on it. <br /><br />That <i>Pan</i> is more fable than love story is alluded to in the name of Glahn’s dog, Aesop, and in the occasional folk tale/myth/legend that is inserted into the story. One recounts the visit by a lover to a woman in her room, another tells of a woman who continues to pine after her lover after he abandons her and locks her up in a tower for forty over years. Its symbolic weight is further underlined (as if it were needed!) by the names of Glahn's two lovers, Eva and Evarda. <br /><br />But if <i>Pan</i> is a fable, then what is it “about”? It is about how the strong are destroyed by the petty and the weak; it is about the strong, virile, masculine force being destroyed by the insidious, cunning, weak, female force. It is not difficult to see how a man who could write this might find the Nazi ideology attractive. Recommendable as a historical curiosity but not much else.</span>
  <a data-text-id="12263332278755261695" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/730754-ken?shelf=finished-in-2019">finished-in-2019</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/730754-ken?shelf=world-literature">world-literature</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer15880127" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer814821538244816993">A cautionary tale: Sometimes it's not a great idea to reread a favorite book from teen days. I can understand why I liked this so much way back when: It features a loner, a hunter living along the coast of Norway, who is in tune with the woods and the stars and the sea. Norway in a Key of Thoreau, in other words. What's not to like?<br/><br/>In the second reading at a much more advantaged (as a reader) age: a lot, turns out. For instance, the jejune back-and-forth between Nimrod type Lieutenant Glahn and </span>
  <span id="freeText814821538244816993" style="display:none">A cautionary tale: Sometimes it's not a great idea to reread a favorite book from teen days. I can understand why I liked this so much way back when: It features a loner, a hunter living along the coast of Norway, who is in tune with the woods and the stars and the sea. Norway in a Key of Thoreau, in other words. What's not to like?<br /><br />In the second reading at a much more advantaged (as a reader) age: a lot, turns out. For instance, the jejune back-and-forth between Nimrod type Lieutenant Glahn and his love interest, still-a-teenager-or-maybe-twenty-tops, Edvarda. Maddening to read.<br /><br />And Glahn himself, once he is taken out of the elemental world of nature and thrust into society, is a clod and an idiot and an embarrassment. In one scene he takes Edvarda's shoe and chucks it in the ocean to get attention. This on a boat filled with partiers who, of course, are shocked.<br /><br />Oh, I admit to laughing when he approached a rival for Edvarda's attention, leaned over, and spit in his ear. It's so over-the-top (and down-the-ear-canal) that it's a bit of a hoot.<br /><br />But after that, things get really dark with Glahn. And reprehensible, as in, you don't go there. You don't do that. And if he does, you probably don't want to read about it.<br /><br />Perhaps this is part of my own evolution as a reader. As I read increasing amounts of poetry and nonfiction, I find myself less patient with the games fiction plays. My eyes roll more and more. My patience stretches thin. If I wore a watch, I'd be looking at it too much while reading books like this.<br /><br />But still, the silver lining: the descriptions of Norway's woods, the life of a loner in a hut surrounded by the myth of a Garden of Eden. Too bad the whole thing's snakebit.</span>
  <a data-text-id="814821538244816993" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_971668180" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/971668180" />
    <a title="RandomAnthony" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/721021-randomanthony"><img alt="RandomAnthony" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1470919631p2/721021.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/971668180?book_show_action=true">Jun 19, 2014</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="RandomAnthony" class="user" itemprop="url" name="RandomAnthony" href="/user/show/721021-randomanthony">RandomAnthony</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer971668180" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer11001771759540365866"><i>Pan</i> is the book that made me understand why Bukowski, Fante, et al revered Knut Hamsun. I don't have the background necessary for pinning Mr. H. on the literary timeline, but I can see, I think, why he's such an important link in the chain.<br/><br/><i>Pan</i>'s narrator is a weird, isolative woodsman with fewer social skills than...than me, even. He extols the joy of his solitude but locks horns, socially and romantically, with a couple town girls. Hamsun's portrayal of the narrator's progression through the to</span>
  <span id="freeText11001771759540365866" style="display:none"><i>Pan</i> is the book that made me understand why Bukowski, Fante, et al revered Knut Hamsun. I don't have the background necessary for pinning Mr. H. on the literary timeline, but I can see, I think, why he's such an important link in the chain.<br /><br /><i>Pan</i>'s narrator is a weird, isolative woodsman with fewer social skills than...than me, even. He extols the joy of his solitude but locks horns, socially and romantically, with a couple town girls. Hamsun's portrayal of the narrator's progression through the town landscape is brilliant. The narrator makes a few painful/hilarious romantic decisions (e.g. hurling a girl's shoe into the sea) and drives himself into a harrowing, simmering rage. An exploration of the conflict/connection between human nature and the surface of social interaction runs through the book. A few sections of <i>Pan</i> were difficult to read; I wanted the narrator to resist impulse and think through his actions. He does, sometimes, I guess, but most often in a way that fails to serve him well. Who hasn't been there? Hamsun's focus on what I suppose I should call psychological realism (to the point where the narrator's perceptions are not trustworthy, I'm sure) is top-notch and, from what I can understand, revolutionary for its time. The fact <i>Pan</i> remains as captivating as ever, over a century after its publication, speaks for its relevance. It's kind of a punk rock book, full of hurt, anger, and fraility. <i>Pan</i>'s required, but not easy, reading. </span>
  <a data-text-id="11001771759540365866" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer657615169" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer4122407117859513220">Loved this -- 4.33 stars. Like a cross between "Hunger" and the more serious/solid/steady <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/1428604.Growth_of_the_Soil' title='Growth of the Soil by Knut Hamsun' rel='nofollow noopener'>Growth of the Soil</a>. Unrealiable narrator in the northlands. One with nature. Loved descriptions of the moon and caterpillars. Super-clear dramatization of romantic tilt-a-whirl caused by trying to anticipate manipulative flipflopping of an attractive capricious lover. Loved the insane, surprising actions -- read them through the filter of Knausgaard's insane actions in <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/16057602.My_Struggle_Book_Two_A_Man_in_Love' title='My Struggle Book Two A Man in Love by Karl Ove Knausgård' rel='nofollow noopener'>My Struggle: Book Two: A Man in Love</a>. </span>
  <span id="freeText4122407117859513220" style="display:none">Loved this -- 4.33 stars. Like a cross between "Hunger" and the more serious/solid/steady <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/1428604.Growth_of_the_Soil" title="Growth of the Soil by Knut Hamsun" rel="nofollow noopener">Growth of the Soil</a>. Unrealiable narrator in the northlands. One with nature. Loved descriptions of the moon and caterpillars. Super-clear dramatization of romantic tilt-a-whirl caused by trying to anticipate manipulative flipflopping of an attractive capricious lover. Loved the insane, surprising actions -- read them through the filter of Knausgaard's insane actions in <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/16057602.My_Struggle_Book_Two_A_Man_in_Love" title="My Struggle Book Two A Man in Love by Karl Ove Knausgård" rel="nofollow noopener">My Struggle: Book Two: A Man in Love</a>. Loved the tiers of lovers -- the meaningless fling, the hopelessly devoted one you love but don't really love, the crazy one who drives you crazy with passion. Loved the triangulations among characters. Loved seeing around the narrator (as they say in creative writing classes). Loved the ending. Oh Glahn, with your animal eyes and gorgeous neck, I laughed out loud when you shot your dog. </span>

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2027965928" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer13487481318420867364">Knut Hamsun’s first novel <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/32585.Hunger' title='Hunger by Knut Hamsun' rel='nofollow noopener'>Hunger</a> (1890) was an immediate experience with a descent into poverty and starvation as the main themes. I did not quite know what to expect as I approached <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/32590.Pan_From_Lieutenant_Thomas_Glahn_s_Papers' title='Pan From Lieutenant Thomas Glahn's Papers by Knut Hamsun' rel='nofollow noopener'>Pan: From Lieutenant Thomas Glahn's Papers</a> (from 1894). However, Hamsun directly grabbed my attention with his naturalism and vivid descriptions of the Norwegian landscape. The main character, Lieutenant Glahn, is a hunter who is intimately linked to the realm of nature, its sounds, cycles, scents and flow. This “pa</span>
  <span id="freeText13487481318420867364" style="display:none">Knut Hamsun’s first novel <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/32585.Hunger" title="Hunger by Knut Hamsun" rel="nofollow noopener">Hunger</a> (1890) was an immediate experience with a descent into poverty and starvation as the main themes. I did not quite know what to expect as I approached <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/32590.Pan_From_Lieutenant_Thomas_Glahn_s_Papers" title="Pan From Lieutenant Thomas Glahn's Papers by Knut Hamsun" rel="nofollow noopener">Pan: From Lieutenant Thomas Glahn's Papers</a> (from 1894). However, Hamsun directly grabbed my attention with his naturalism and vivid descriptions of the Norwegian landscape. The main character, Lieutenant Glahn, is a hunter who is intimately linked to the realm of nature, its sounds, cycles, scents and flow. This “pastoral” setting shifts by the slow ascent of a relationship with a young girl, Edvarda, from a merchant family in the nearby town. The dynamics of this relationship form the core of the novel. The psychology and shifting ripples of the relationship shape and form the story as it progresses. It was complex, as I struggled to understand Edvarda’s psyche. It is definitely a rollercoaster in terms of the interaction between the two main characters. In addition, there is an almost supernatural presence that I cannot quite put my finger on. It is almost as if Hamsun wants to bring in this flavor into the novel, but leaves it in the realm of mystery. Can one sense Pan dancing and playing in the forest? Are the main characters enchanted by the music they cannot "hear"?<br><br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1503588826i/23678181.jpg" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><br>Now, in retrospect, I sense a tug of war with Glahn’s intimate link to nature with the growing attachment with a girl from “civilization”. For some odd reason, the epic Gilgamesh popped up into my mind as I started thinking about the hero’s friend Enkidu’s shift between worlds as he encountered a woman for the first time. This story seems quite similar in terms of reality shifts. Sverre Lyngstad’s translation is vivid and one never really senses that this work was originally written in Norwegian. It flows very nicely and the story unfolds at a steady pace. There were a few events towards the end of the novel that seemed farfetched, but I suspect that they could be symbols of a shift of realities. They simply contrasted with the previous psychological drama between the two main characters. <br><br>I found Hamsun’s work enchanting as it somehow marries the dynamics of a complex psychological dance wrapped in the realm of nature. It has the scent of a Russian novel soaked in the Norwegian landscape with a touch of Ibsen. Highly recommended as it definitely gives one the incentive to explore additional works of Hamsun as well as Scandinavian literature overall.<br><br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1503588381i/23678120._SX540_.png" class="gr-hostedUserImg"></span>
  <a data-text-id="13487481318420867364" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_161362426" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/161362426" />
    <a title="Moon Rose" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/5189565-moon-rose"><img alt="Moon Rose" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1597367219p2/5189565.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/161362426?book_show_action=true">Apr 15, 2011</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Moon Rose" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Moon Rose" href="/user/show/5189565-moon-rose">Moon Rose</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Kindle Edition " href="/book/show/9037725-pan">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5189565-moon-rose?shelf=fiction">fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5189565-moon-rose?shelf=nobel-laureates">nobel-laureates</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5189565-moon-rose?shelf=favorite-books-of-all-time">favorite-books-of-all-time</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5189565-moon-rose?shelf=favorite-writers">favorite-writers</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5189565-moon-rose?shelf=2011-shelf">2011-shelf</a>
          </div>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer161362426" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer6783488440749719006"> Pan is a love story on a different take---dark, tragic, strange and twisted. It shows a peculiar knack of Knut Hamsun to discern the capricious, often unstable human personalities in its most intrinsic fashion as it collides with one another, giving form to the abstract aspects of the human experiences, thus creating uniquely drawn characters and a story that bursts with originality, which give further definition to his already profound talent as a writer. Hamsun's vivid depiction of human expe</span>
  <span id="freeText6783488440749719006" style="display:none"> Pan is a love story on a different take---dark, tragic, strange and twisted. It shows a peculiar knack of Knut Hamsun to discern the capricious, often unstable human personalities in its most intrinsic fashion as it collides with one another, giving form to the abstract aspects of the human experiences, thus creating uniquely drawn characters and a story that bursts with originality, which give further definition to his already profound talent as a writer. Hamsun's vivid depiction of human experience is two folds: man's relation with love, as man tries to struggle from its grip that often brings about the self that is unknown to him and man's unique relation with one another, as it shows how people affect and mould each other's character and influence each other's lives. The enormity of his imaginative flare lies in his ability to subtlety shape a character in a novel, though in a smaller scope as this, with less space, he succeeds to convey to the reader its authenticity as it becomes real as real life itself, as his hero faces waves and waves of harmless experiences that appear only like ripples from beyond but in a moment's time turn turbulent as it lays its devastating arms unto the shore. Thomas Glahn, a handsome young man who somehow found solace in his isolation, a secluded life of peaceful union with nature and seems to be on the verge of self discovery in his solitude was suddenly pulled away from his solemn reverie by the appearance of a young capricious girl, Edwarda, an idealist with distorted views, who gradually digressed the course of his given destiny that led to his eventual tragic end.<br /> </span>
  <a data-text-id="6783488440749719006" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer88262955" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer11673357923036582631">I wish I had read it a little quicker. I put this down about half way in, some other reading got in the way, and so it took a while to read and the momentum was kinda lost. Nevertheless... a great book, he has a way of creating the strangest voices that are not simple parodies, but are very funny and effective at the same time. There is a lot going on in here beyond the voice, much more going on here than in Hunger actually (though it might take more patience than that book, as there are many pa</span>
  <span id="freeText11673357923036582631" style="display:none">I wish I had read it a little quicker. I put this down about half way in, some other reading got in the way, and so it took a while to read and the momentum was kinda lost. Nevertheless... a great book, he has a way of creating the strangest voices that are not simple parodies, but are very funny and effective at the same time. There is a lot going on in here beyond the voice, much more going on here than in Hunger actually (though it might take more patience than that book, as there are many passages of very little action). The infatuation and the mind's going back and forth is a similar element, but here it is more muted, and I think more complex. The juxtaposition of the last section, where a different speaker talks about the first speaker is a nice touch as well. This book makes me think, a lot. I don't really know if I can talk about it intelligently, yet. The main characters go through many subtle and violent changes, almost completely out of their own control or understanding (and mine); I pity them. </span>
  <a data-text-id="11673357923036582631" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

<div id="review_898916651" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/898916651" />
    <a title="Aric Cushing" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/26050926-aric-cushing"><img alt="Aric Cushing" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1466785748p2/26050926.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/898916651?book_show_action=true">Apr 02, 2014</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Aric Cushing" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Aric Cushing" href="/user/show/26050926-aric-cushing">Aric Cushing</a>
      </span>

          added it




        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer898916651" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer11008561092353173766">A strange tale of a huntsman in Norway whose odd, sick behavior seems revelatory in light of his brief love affair with a local girl. Few books ever touch on this kind of character, or this kind of depiction of human actions brought on by uncontrollable emotin. Reminds one of Death in Venice. DEFINITELY worth the read.</span>

          </span>
        </div>

    <a title="Steve" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/17433080-steve"><img alt="Steve" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1420185854p2/17433080.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">

        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">

              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/17433080-steve?shelf=norwegian">norwegian</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer555159184" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer1492402878093351292">Since there are so many reviews of this book I shall record merely that when I first read this book in college, I was not prepared to appreciate its beautiful strangeness. Hamsun mixes together provincial mundanity in northernmost Norway, Nature mysteries, the pettiness, vindictiveness and hurtfulness of much of human behavior and a main character who moves back and forth between sanity and madness without noticing, whose primary love interest seems intent on matching his erratic and impulsive/c</span>
  <span id="freeText1492402878093351292" style="display:none">Since there are so many reviews of this book I shall record merely that when I first read this book in college, I was not prepared to appreciate its beautiful strangeness. Hamsun mixes together provincial mundanity in northernmost Norway, Nature mysteries, the pettiness, vindictiveness and hurtfulness of much of human behavior and a main character who moves back and forth between sanity and madness without noticing, whose primary love interest seems intent on matching his erratic and impulsive/compulsive acts step for step and who finally longs for death. This is not a recounting of a young love doomed by social differences, as I have seen <i> Pan </i> described in various places, any more than <i> Moby Dick </i> is a yarn about a fish. </span>
  <a data-text-id="1492402878093351292" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer68062686" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer14651260861957433506">I would call Glahn, our narrator, a capricious wanker-- but then he really is very human and pitiable, like the rest of us. His mind always seems to be nested in what he lost rather then what he has before him. The story leaps as his mind does, between the female characters who, either physical or in dream form (Iselind), are the bane of his tortured existence. <br/>The setting is interesting - during summer in Norway, the further north you go, the more light there is during night-time, I imagine the</span>
  <span id="freeText14651260861957433506" style="display:none">I would call Glahn, our narrator, a capricious wanker-- but then he really is very human and pitiable, like the rest of us. His mind always seems to be nested in what he lost rather then what he has before him. The story leaps as his mind does, between the female characters who, either physical or in dream form (Iselind), are the bane of his tortured existence. <br />The setting is interesting - during summer in Norway, the further north you go, the more light there is during night-time, I imagine the senses are somewhat disrupted. That is the only way I can explain the haze surrounding the narrative, and the odd reactions of the characters.<br />It's a very readable, peculiar and wonderful novel.</span>
  <a data-text-id="14651260861957433506" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<div id="review_2676194451" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2676194451" />
    <a title="Michael Canoeist" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/2179154-michael-canoeist"><img alt="Michael Canoeist" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1241128223p2/2179154.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2676194451?book_show_action=true">Jan 16, 2019</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Michael Canoeist" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Michael Canoeist" href="/user/show/2179154-michael-canoeist">Michael Canoeist</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2179154-michael-canoeist?shelf=norway">norway</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2179154-michael-canoeist?shelf=hamsun">hamsun</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2179154-michael-canoeist?shelf=romance">romance</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2676194451" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer12492477566388833095"><i>Pan</i>, published in 1894 by the confounding Knut Hamsun, is my favorite of his early novels. These are intense, subjective, individualistic stories told within an aura of nature, mystery, love, beauty and conflicts. This love story is 188 pages in my edition, a mid-20th-century translation by James McFarlane, and to me it is irresistible despite the painfulness of all the crossed signals, contradictions, and rejections that happen along the way. <br/><br/>Hamsun grew up in Nordland [pictured <a target='_blank' rel='noopener nofollow' href='https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/74/Ballstad_Lofoten.JPG/1280px-Ballstad_Lofoten.JPG'>https://upload.</a></span>
  <span id="freeText12492477566388833095" style="display:none"><i>Pan</i>, published in 1894 by the confounding Knut Hamsun, is my favorite of his early novels. These are intense, subjective, individualistic stories told within an aura of nature, mystery, love, beauty and conflicts. This love story is 188 pages in my edition, a mid-20th-century translation by James McFarlane, and to me it is irresistible despite the painfulness of all the crossed signals, contradictions, and rejections that happen along the way. <br /><br />Hamsun grew up in Nordland [pictured <a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/74/Ballstad_Lofoten.JPG/1280px-Ballstad_Lofoten.JPG">https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedi...</a>], a region halfway up Norway, part of which is above the Arctic Circle, yet with a surprisingly moderate climate. Those super-long summer days play a big role in this book, as the opening sentences tell us: <br /><br />"These last few days I have thought and thought of the Nordland summer's endless day. I sit here and think of it, and of a hut I lived in, and of the forest behind the hut; and I have taken to writing about it, just for my own amusement and to while away the time. Time drags; it does not pass as quickly as I should like, although I have no cares and lead the gayest of lives. I am perfectly content with everything, and thirty is no great age. A few days ago, I received a couple of bird's feathers from far away, from one who need not have sent them; just two green feathers folded in a sheet of paper with a coronet on it and fastened with a seal. It amused me to see such fiendishly green feathers. Otherwise there is nothing to trouble me except a touch of arthritis now and then in my left foot, the result of an old shot wound that healed up long ago."<br /><br />I have read <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/764431.Pan" title="Pan by Knut Hamsun" rel="nofollow noopener">Pan</a> several times at different stages in life. The experience has been remarkably consistent, a beautiful, painful, uplifting, downcasting, privileged set of moments. It is young love set in an unusual place; but much more than that, too, somehow. It is also the story of the stranger who wants to fit into a new, small society he finds himself in; and can almost do it.</span>
  <a data-text-id="12492477566388833095" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_50302449" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/50302449" />
    <a title="Alex V." class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/334560-alex-v"><img alt="Alex V." src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1442608235p2/334560.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/50302449?book_show_action=true">Mar 24, 2009</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Alex V." class="user" itemprop="url" name="Alex V." href="/user/show/334560-alex-v">Alex V.</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer50302449" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer568743160966096914">Of all the stuff I read in college, nothing lasted longer with me than Knut Hamsun's Hunger, and with stops and starts over the years I've tried his other books without ever finishing them. Then I read somewhere that John Fante got the title or the idea for Ask the Dust, one of my favorite books ever, from Hamsun's Pan, and that was enough for me.<br/><br/>This is a spectacular little book, a hair over 100 pages in the lovely edition I got form the library complete with woodcut illustrations, documenting </span>
  <span id="freeText568743160966096914" style="display:none">Of all the stuff I read in college, nothing lasted longer with me than Knut Hamsun's Hunger, and with stops and starts over the years I've tried his other books without ever finishing them. Then I read somewhere that John Fante got the title or the idea for Ask the Dust, one of my favorite books ever, from Hamsun's Pan, and that was enough for me.<br /><br />This is a spectacular little book, a hair over 100 pages in the lovely edition I got form the library complete with woodcut illustrations, documenting a season of a narcissist spends hunting in the frozen wastes of upper Norway and how the hearts of the people burn through all that ice. The protagonist is almost categorically unsympathetic, the dialog a little antiquated, the secondary characters a little one-dimensional, yet it all comes together as the way the world does in the mind, pieces cut large and kicked into place so that they fit in our own particular puzzles.<br /><br />Knut Hamsun seems to have been a terrible person, a Nazi sympathizer so pronounced that in 1943 he sent Goebbels his 1920 Nobel Prize medal as a gift. And yet, in Pan, he reconciles uncomfortable people in a harrowing landscape and reveals the richness of their humanity, the common spark that illuminates us all from within. </span>
  <a data-text-id="568743160966096914" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_76668543" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/76668543" />

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/76668543?book_show_action=true">Nov 03, 2009</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Andrew" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Andrew" href="/user/show/442654-andrew">Andrew</a>
      </span>

          added it




        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/442654-andrew?shelf=norwegian-fiction">norwegian-fiction</a>,
